  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_714da1d3-5d3b-4b31-91e6-ed250635ec75'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16552889687127520141">This is what we dream of: to be so swept away, so poleaxed by a book that the breath is sucked right out of us. Brace yourselves.<br/> <br/>May 1565. Suleiman the Magnificent, emperor of the Ottomans, has declared a jihad against the Knights of Saint John the Baptist.   <span id="freeText16552889687127520141" style="display:none">This is what we dream of: to be so swept away, so poleaxed by a book that the breath is sucked right out of us. Brace yourselves.<br /> <br />May 1565. Suleiman the Magnificent, emperor of the Ottomans, has declared a jihad against the Knights of Saint John the Baptist. The largest armada of all time approaches the knights' Christian stronghold on the island of Malta. The Turks know the knights as the "Hounds of Hell." The knights call themselves "The Religion."<br /> <br />In Messina, Sicily, a French countess, Carla La Penautier, seeks passage to Malta in a quest to find the son taken from her at his birth twelve years ago. The only man with the expertise and daring to help her is a Rabelaisian soldier of fortune, arms dealer, former janissary, and strapping Saxon adventurer by the name of Mattias Tannhauser. He agrees to accompany the lady to Malta, where, amid the most spectacular siege in military history, they must try to find the boy--whose name they do not know and whose face they have never seen--and pluck him from the jaws of Holy War.<br /> <br />The Religion is the first book of the Tannhauser Trilogy, and from the first page of this epic account of the last great medieval conflict between East and West, it is clear we are in the hands of a master. Not since James Clavell has a novelist so powerfully and assuredly plunged readers headlong into another world and time. Anne Rice transformed the vampire novel. Stephen King reinvented horror. Now, in a spectacular tale of heroism, tragedy, and passion, Tim Willocks revivifies historical fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16552889687127520141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_520393545" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/520393545" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/520393545?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780765357557" href="/book/show/1277511.The_Religion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=middle-ages">middle-ages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=creeps-perverts-psychos-killers">creeps-perverts-psychos-killers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer520393545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14575147628263910356">I guess <i>The Religion</i> is a good title...it's cryptic and kinda ominous sounding and does have a connection to the actual contents of the story. Despite that, I think that <i>Fucking, Killing & Shitting: Malta 1565</i> might be a bit more appropriate here. While I can certainly agree all three of those things are an unavoidable part of life and can be an enjoyable part of fiction, I really don't think that's what Mr. Willocks was intending for me to see as the identity of his story--but that's what I cam</span>
  <span id="freeText14575147628263910356" style="display:none">I guess <i>The Religion</i> is a good title...it's cryptic and kinda ominous sounding and does have a connection to the actual contents of the story. Despite that, I think that <i>Fucking, Killing &amp; Shitting: Malta 1565</i> might be a bit more appropriate here. While I can certainly agree all three of those things are an unavoidable part of life and can be an enjoyable part of fiction, I really don't think that's what Mr. Willocks was intending for me to see as the identity of his story--but that's what I came away with. The book doesn't seem to know whether it wants to be a thoughtful, profound telling of this event or a lurid historical novel packed with cheap thrills.<br /><br />It sucks, because the author certainly writes well. His prose is pretty and can be ornate, even. He's done his homework and writes the setting and events vividly and with authority. The siege of Malta was more like a grueling, epic battle fought over the span of a few months and Willocks certainly paints it well. You can see the banners swaying and the massed white hats of the Janissaries and hear the cry of the war horns with ease and very little force of imagination, which is always, always a great litmus test for writers of historical novels. It's easy to see where Willocks is strong. Once you get a bit deeper than that, it becomes more problematic.<br /><br />The characters are the first issue of this confusing book. The main character is Matthias Tannhauser, a Saxon dude who saw his family slain when he was a child then promptly joined their murderers--the Ottoman Empire. Eventually he comes to employment as badass gun smuggler or whatever. It all sounds cool, but the man himself is relatively boring. He's hardly flawed--Willocks makes a point of his creepy love of killing the absolute shit out of the Ottomans, but that's it--and is very typical anti-hero with his "playing both sides" thing. Then there's Bors, his likable English companion. This is what I'm talking about--you don't have main characters that can be summed up as "likable English companion" in the kind of novel that Willocks seemed to occasionally be striving for. <br /><br />Next we have Carla, who I actually liked. She seems to be more human than the rest of the cast and I loved the scenes of her taking care of the multitude of injured in the hospital. I'm not a Christian, but I find generalized and bullheaded anti-Christian rhetoric irritating and boring as much as I do generalized and bullheaded pro-Christian rhetoric, so it was nice to see piety rendered as a positive character trait in such a modern-thinking novel filled with the baser parts of life written by someone I'm guessing to be a fellow heathen. The conflict between her pious and erotic natures was an interesting part of her personality. Piety as interpreted by the Inquisition as written by Willocks--not as nice, however. The main antagonist here is Ludovico Ludovici (I'm pretty sure that was his name, and I did laugh writing that out) who is a former inquisitor and burner. He's a pretty decent bad guy--Willocks makes the effort of making him a human being gone wrong and not an innately evil villain. <br /><br />Then we have Amparo, whose presence as a character was completely mystifying and aggravating for me. She's Carla's best friend and maidservant...her family was predictably killed when she was a baby in a horrible way and she went through hell until she met Carla or whatever. She has this aloof yet passionately intense kind of personality that no human in fucking history ever had. She predictably falls in love with Tannhauser and serves as his sex doll for the rest of the book, stopping occasionally to play with virtuoso skill on violin. It's just all so...dumb. Any time she was on the page if she wasn't having some inane overwrought thought process she was having ridiculous sex with Tannhauser that serves to further the incredibly dull love triangle. Who has sex in a fucking WOODEN TUB OF BRINE? Dumb and even offensive in its dumbness. People have sex and realistically drawn adult characters in fiction should have sex, but not like this. I know this rant probably makes me sound like a prick, but her whole existence really was an incredibly annoying invention by the author.<br /><br />Anyways...three paragraphs of ranting about the lukewarm characters. I'm sure that's a joy to read...that was basically the whole issue with this book--the back and forth between something more concrete and profound and just an orgy of violence, defecation and sex. A note about the poop content: I think that more authors need to include the fact that their characters have to use the bathroom. It's not a pleasant part of life but it certainly brings a gross realism to the book. I remember the characters shat a lot in <i>Shogun</i> and I thought it was cool Clavell had the balls to include it. The thing is I could probably enjoy both of these possible books; the garbage cheap-thriller or the insightful epic. But the schizophrenic nature of both combined was a huge problem for me. I don't know, maybe this kind of species of book should exist anyways--one straddling the line between trash and true substance. I liked it--but I could have liked it a lot more, easily. Or maybe I'm just being pretentious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14575147628263910356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_81965207" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81965207" />
    <a title="Karla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2846441-karla"><img alt="Karla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537371209p2/2846441.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81965207?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karla" href="/user/show/2846441-karla">Karla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780765357557" href="/book/show/1277511.The_Religion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2846441-karla?shelf=dead-tree">dead-tree</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81965207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9472998756023404995">I hated this book. I love violence and don't mind sex in my fiction, but this book was absolutely wretched, despite having those two ingredients. The main fault, I think, was the hero. He was too perfect and was therefore absolutely obnoxious. He knows how everything should be defended, he is looked to grudgingly for his experience in the Muslim world, he turns the loins of women to water and the hearts to men to envy. And unfortunately there are precious few scenes where he doesn't suck out the</span>
  <span id="freeText9472998756023404995" style="display:none">I hated this book. I love violence and don't mind sex in my fiction, but this book was absolutely wretched, despite having those two ingredients. The main fault, I think, was the hero. He was too perfect and was therefore absolutely obnoxious. He knows how everything should be defended, he is looked to grudgingly for his experience in the Muslim world, he turns the loins of women to water and the hearts to men to envy. And unfortunately there are precious few scenes where he doesn't suck out the oxygen with his arrogance and "Dig me!" attitude. When he's not sassing Inquisitors, he's bending his concubine over an anvil and making her wonder how she could ever be luckier.<br /><br />The female characters were atrocious. One, Amparo, is a borderline idiot and sperm receptacle (namely the hero's), and the noblewoman Carla is a frail flower whose only moment of steel occurs when she's channeling Jesus in the hospital. Everywhere else, she's imagining herself in Amparo's place getting peerlessly reamed by our randy hero.<br /><br />The rest of the cast (for this book has "blockbuster" scrawled all over it) is peppered with stereotypes and cliches. There's Bors, the burly and gruff sidekick tailor-made for Ray Winstone or Brendon Gleeson in Yorkshire overdrive. Ludovico was your typical evil priest who lapses into lust and brings woe unto all who cross him. There were others, but everyone pretty much was overshadowed by his Badassedness Mattias Tannhauser.<br /><br />And then there was the feces. Holy crap! It's no lie to say this was the sh!ttiest book I've ever read. Literally. Practically every 5th page, there was some mention of sight, smell, or the act of evacuating one's bowels. (The part during Sabato Svi's torture was my favorite, when Tannhauser is fighting one of the baddies and "panic squealed forth" from said baddie's arse. My #1 LOLarious line of 2009.) Whether it was in battle, a streetfight, a hospital, or just passing an alley, the subject of excrement came up with full-on detail. It was so bizarrely repetitive and single-minded. Just what does Mr. Willocks get up to in his spare time? Are glass coffee tables involved?<br /><br />There's a plot in there somewhere about finding Carla's &amp; Ludovico's long-lost son bred in mutual love/lust, but it was swamped by swiving, long-winded speeches, purple prose, and characters that were as dull on page 100 as they were 500+ pages later. I went into the book with reasonable expectations and left utterly disappointed that only a couple of my tormenters were killed by the end and baffled that the ALA thought this was noteworthy.<br /><br />I have no problem with my carps being in old bodice rippers. In fact, I love the un-PC nature of those books with troglodyte heroes and less-than-steely-spined heroines, but historical fiction is supposed to be better. In fact, we're often told that. Then be "better." And when it wins an award, it better be "better."</span>
  <a data-text-id="9472998756023404995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_492931789" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/492931789" />
    <a title="Terri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1968826-terri"><img alt="Terri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356560426p2/1968826.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/492931789?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terri" href="/user/show/1968826-terri">Terri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099581291" href="/book/show/17307643-the-religion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=europe-fiction">europe-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer492931789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18154883399492253823"> There are a lot of rare things in this world that I would like to get my hands on. Pink diamonds. Vintage John Paul Gaultier Corset Dresses. Black Limited Edition Burberry Trenchcoats. An Aston Martin Vantage (V8 or V12, I am not fussy) and.... lengthy books that stay consistent in pace and quality from front cover to back cover. <br/><br/>Obviously, for me, only one of these was ever going to be attainable. And it wasn't the Aston Martin Vantage. It was, of course, a lengthy book with pace and quality f</span>
  <span id="freeText18154883399492253823" style="display:none"> There are a lot of rare things in this world that I would like to get my hands on. Pink diamonds. Vintage John Paul Gaultier Corset Dresses. Black Limited Edition Burberry Trenchcoats. An Aston Martin Vantage (V8 or V12, I am not fussy) and.... lengthy books that stay consistent in pace and quality from front cover to back cover. <br /><br />Obviously, for me, only one of these was ever going to be attainable. And it wasn't the Aston Martin Vantage. It was, of course, a lengthy book with pace and quality finish to end.<br /><br /> An enigma. A myth. Often whispered about, never actually seen. I have tried one or two, been tempted by a promise of cover to cover bounty many times. Was once wrongly directed down the Count of Monte Cristo path. A book that I felt suffered from much the same problem as many lengthy books. Too much waffle and could benefit from being a couple hundred pages shorter. Okay, so editors were less keen on cutting back then, but they should not be too scared of it now. And yet they seem to be.<br /><br />I will concede that The Religion could have been shorter. Only nothing drastic. 50 pages at the most. But a few too many pages was kind of a small price to pay for a book that did not run out of prose in the first few chapters. It went on. On and on. On and on and on. Beefed up with excellent landscape and character creation, anchored by vivid portals into extraordinary battle scenes. <br />I could not put it down. <br />It was a gem as rare as any pink diamond, with as much pace at times as any V12 Aston Martin. As tight at the top as at the waist as at the back, as any Gaultier corset and with more movement than any Burberry trench.<br />It was quite a find.<br />A rare find that you will notice from the outset. With passages that wash across the page in fresh, vernal literary splashes.<br /><br />Page 12<br />The Fagaras Mountains, East Hungarian Marches. Spring AD1540<br /><i><br />The yard was empty. The heavens at the rimrock's edge were reefed in vermilion cloud. From the village pillars of smoke quavered skyward and with them cries of anguish and crackles of flame. <br />He walked across the cobbles, sick with fear. Fear of whatever vileness afflicted his mother. Fear of shame. Of cowardice. Of the knowledge that he couldn't save her. Of the darkness that had housed itself inside his spirit. Yet the darkness spoke with a feral power that brooked no refusal nor hesitation.</i><br />Plunge in, the darkness said.<br /><i>Mattias turned and looked back at the forge...</i><br />Like the blade in the quench.<br />Plunge in.<br />***<br /> <br />Through to the middle they surged on. The tautness of a well trained writer carving his skill in inked words.<br /> <br />Page 348<br />The Gauntlet – The Bailey – The Causeway. 11 June 1565<br /><i><br />Straighten up, breathe and blow, shake the sweat. He wheezed. His chest was tight, his gorge scorched. He felt nauseous and weak. He was too far forward. Get back.<br />The horde shouldered each other in their frenzy to get through the choke point, their weapons constricted, one shield obstructing another. Spot the openings. Swallow the scalding bile. Kill him, kill them, kill them all. A blow glanced off his helm and hammered into his pauldron. Spike him in the privities, stab him in the neck. The fellow fought on from his knees, blinded by the fountain from his arteries, still scrabbling with his blade for the joints in Tannhauser's plate.<br />Tannhauser drove the finial through his temple and stepped back. Now backstep again. Keep them at bay. He threw an upward swordcut to the thighs and backstroke to the guts and a thrust to the chest, in deep and twist. Don't look in his eyes. He's done. And breathe, you fool, keep the knees loose, ignore the battle cries. Get back.</i><br /><br />It goes on. On and on and on. But I think I have shown you enough.<br /><br />Don't be dissuaded from trying this for fear it is too masculine. It is masculine, without a doubt, but there is beauty too. Beauty of the heart and of the lovelorn. The author has not forgotten you. You, the reader of the heart.<br />While I did not think there was much romance in this book, it is there. A tugging undertow that a reader like me - who does not read with the heart but reads with the mind - can easily ignore. I hardly even noticed the female characters most of the time. They did not take up much room in my mental landscape.<br /><br />To me the book is perfect. Perfectly written. Fast paced from start to finish. <br />The story, however, had some personal taste flaws. They are not going to be flaws for everyone. Some of what I call a flaw, will be the things you will most likely value about the story.<br />It was, ahem, the sex. <br />Some may say it had to be there as sex is a natural part of life. In part this is true (although many live without sex in their lives and I would never claim their lives to be unnaturally led), but the sex in this book is quite often odd. Poorly located. Unnecessary. Forced into the story.<br />There isn't a lot of it and if you blink - or skim read - you may even miss it. <br />When it does come (no pun intended) it is oddly placed, like an afterthought, or to please the authors own building sexual tension. <br />If an author wants sex in a book it needs to feel naturally placed. Not just plopped down because the author was randy or the editor told him it needed more sex.<br /><br />It was a great ride while it lasted (the book, not the sex) and I was disappointed it came to an end. My forlorn need not last forever though, for there is a book to follow it called Twelve Children of Paris. I have bought it already and cannot wait to read it. <br />With any luck, it will be as well done as this rare gem, The Religion. As this kind of writing skill is not something an author loses down a bottle of whiskey while he tries to outdrink his writer's block. No, he clearly has command of his writing talent.<br />The only place I feel he can fall down will be story. Lets hope he gets that right again too.<br /><br />Due to the odd sex. 4 1/2 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18154883399492253823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131573455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7996075173579808132">This is the first book I've read in years that I felt was NOT written at a 6th grade level. Willocks' writing is like reading poetry. Even though in this particular book the "poetry" is about knights hacking each other to death on mounds of dead bodies. I felt like I was reading a book written at an adult level. No, not smutty, just smart and intelligent. <br/><br/>I'm not putting much here regarding the subject or plot other than the Knights I've mentioned. But if you like stories about Knights Templar A</span>
  <span id="freeText7996075173579808132" style="display:none">This is the first book I've read in years that I felt was NOT written at a 6th grade level. Willocks' writing is like reading poetry. Even though in this particular book the "poetry" is about knights hacking each other to death on mounds of dead bodies. I felt like I was reading a book written at an adult level. No, not smutty, just smart and intelligent. <br /><br />I'm not putting much here regarding the subject or plot other than the Knights I've mentioned. But if you like stories about Knights Templar AND you like (VERY) well written literature, pick up this book or any others by Willocks, and you'll be well pleased. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7996075173579808132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_10917441" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10917441" />
    <a title="eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/697321-eric"><img alt="eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1198032586p2/697321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10917441?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="eric" href="/user/show/697321-eric">eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15326532664689130946">I was really disappointed with this book. It's essentially a Harlequin romance with a heavy dose of graphic violence.   <span id="freeText15326532664689130946" style="display:none">I was really disappointed with this book. It's essentially a Harlequin romance with a heavy dose of graphic violence. Willocks is clearly a very bright man, but his writing is schmaltzy, to say the least. Try this on for size:<br /><br />&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;<br />[Tannhauser compliments Carla]<br />Carla felt her cheeks burn. She felt inadequate to the compliment and to acknowledge it seemed improper. A sense of sin clenched inside her. Such fears and doubts had hedged her life for as long as she could remember. Yet in these few moments he'd blown through all that dust like a wind throught a long unopened room.<br /><br />She said, "Do you believe in magic?"<br />&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;<br /><br />The characters are cliche to the extreme. The protagonist, Tannhauser, is a battled-scarred, highly skilled warrior who has sworn off violence until his friends and a damsel are threatened. Yet there is darkness and emotional vulnerability lurking just underneath his hard appearancee. How many times have we seen this?<br /><br />If you really like mawkish romance and violence, then you'll likely enjoy this book. Otherwise, I'd suggest reading something else.</span>

<div id="review_1331621118" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1331621118" />
    <a title="John Wiltshire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31591928-john-wiltshire"><img alt="John Wiltshire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442976862p2/31591928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1331621118?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Wiltshire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Wiltshire" href="/user/show/31591928-john-wiltshire">John Wiltshire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1331621118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer348024082820809143">I came to this novel from the author's outstanding work <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/694270.Green_River_Rising' title='Green River Rising by Tim Willocks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Green River Rising</a>, which is one of the most memorable books I've ever read. Haunting hardly captures the effect it had on me. So when I read that this was a book about the Knights of St John defending Malta by the same author who wrote such a powerful novel about a contemporary prison riot I was intrigued. And how timely, a novel about the defence of European civilization. Yes, this is the 1500s, but times, apparently, don't change all tha</span>
  <span id="freeText348024082820809143" style="display:none">I came to this novel from the author's outstanding work <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/694270.Green_River_Rising" title="Green River Rising by Tim Willocks" rel="nofollow noopener">Green River Rising</a>, which is one of the most memorable books I've ever read. Haunting hardly captures the effect it had on me. So when I read that this was a book about the Knights of St John defending Malta by the same author who wrote such a powerful novel about a contemporary prison riot I was intrigued. And how timely, a novel about the defence of European civilization. Yes, this is the 1500s, but times, apparently, don't change all that much. <br />I'm 11% in and have very mixed feelings about the book. Some of it is superb, but it's extremely verbose and overblown as well. Bodices and swooning and golden stallions and crimson dresses and beryl eyes and ... Game of Thrones on steroids, and that's pretty exhausting to read at times. But for all that I'm quite caught up in the tale and will persevere. <br />I'll update when done.<br />20% in and I'm not letting Radulf read this book. A whole heartbreaking chapter about the slaughter of the dogs on Malta before the siege. The Head of the Order of St John determined that the barbarians would not reduce them to eating dogs and rats, and so to prevent this he calls for a total cull of dogs on the island. His own beloved hunting dogs killed by his own hand first. It's almost too painful to read, but such powerful writing too. I am seriously caught up in this story now, possibly real life mirroring fiction mirroring real life a little too much for comfort though. The brave Christian defenders of the tiny army are facing overwhelming odds...<br />60% in now and so grateful I gave this book a chance. My mother lived in Malta as a child; my father was stationed there for many years, and I went there in my early 20s, so I have extremely fond feelings for the island. I haven't read anything that has stirred my blood like this book has for quite a long time. I've never read such accurate, honest, brutal descriptions of war in any other novels, and I've made a bit of a study of military books. Brutality, yes, beyond comprehension, but where this book is so astounding is the way the author also captures the transcendent bravery, the heroism, the glory of it all that has drawn men to war in every age. And to their religions, come to that. I highly recommend this novel to anyone who watched the Lord of the Rings movies (or read the books) and really only enjoyed the epic battles such as at Helm's Deep and Minas Tirith and wished those scenes could have gone on for longer. This novel is thousands of pages of a siege that makes Helm's Deep read like a walk in the park. Sure, there are quieter moments with some women doing boring things with musical instruments and dresses, but if you skim those pages the Knights of St John will steal your heart (or make you wish you'd never left the army and could rejoin). <br />I'll update when fully done.<br />Finished, and very glad I read this amazing novel. Highly recommened.</span>
  <a data-text-id="348024082820809143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10417939877645302976" style="display:none"> This was most interesting as to the time period -- 16th century -- Knights of St. John [Hospitallers] enduring the siege by the Ottoman Turks under Suleiman the Magnificent on the Island of Malta. Enter our protagonist, Mattias Tannhauser, the larger-than-life picaresque hero and soldier-of-fortune. As a former Janissary, Tannhauser can inflitrate the Turkish camp as necessary. The battle scenes were very good; however they were much too graphic in places -- too much mention of body parts and bodily functions. I learned much of the Janissaries and their way of life. I was glad to see that not every Turk was presented as a villain. The conclusion of the book was most satisfying. This is the first novel of a projected trilogy on Tannhauser. I feel that any sequels will be anticlimactic and unnecessary. </span>

<div id="review_2006102135" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2006102135" />
    <a title="Adam Nevill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22902860-adam-nevill"><img alt="Adam Nevill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465841872p2/22902860.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2006102135?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam Nevill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam Nevill" href="/user/show/22902860-adam-nevill">Adam Nevill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2006102135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4797109333689535915">Book Recs: Extended action scenes, battle scenes, meaningful depictions of large scale violence, can be hard to depict in prose. I don't tend to enjoy reading them; maybe the screen is a better medium in this area. But there are some masterly literary practitioners of combat on a large scale (Abercrombie, McCarthy, Doctorow spring to mind). Willocks is another writer who excels in this area, and not only does he excel at depicting the appalling savagery of historical warfare, he also excels at r</span>
  <span id="freeText4797109333689535915" style="display:none">Book Recs: Extended action scenes, battle scenes, meaningful depictions of large scale violence, can be hard to depict in prose. I don't tend to enjoy reading them; maybe the screen is a better medium in this area. But there are some masterly literary practitioners of combat on a large scale (Abercrombie, McCarthy, Doctorow spring to mind). Willocks is another writer who excels in this area, and not only does he excel at depicting the appalling savagery of historical warfare, he also excels at recreating history and making the past, and its citizens, live so vividly.<br /><br />Two epic stories here, often heartbreaking, with excruciating inner struggles that characters endure between baseness and grace. 'The Religion' probably remains one of my favourite novels. I read it on publication in 2006. It takes The Siege of Malta (1565) as its subject. Reading it left me feeling physically and mentally fatigued. A desert island book for me. Not long finished 'The Twelve Children of Paris'. Also a masterpiece imo, and based on the St Bartholomew's Day Massacre in Paris (1572); this one requires a strong stomach. Particularly poignant in a world still addicted to sectarian violence and both novels left me relieved that I was born in the late 1960s and not the 1560s. Each story completely transported me as a reader.<br /><br />If you like grim but marvellous adventures of humanity in times of inhumanity, these are the first two books in the Tannhauser trilogy. I look forward to the third installment. One of the finest living British writers that I've read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4797109333689535915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8051137213512087241">I am torn between giving four stars and five. The language and vocabulary are exquisite. It is only the incessant warring that makes one weary in the reading. The slaying and butchery would have been more striking had it not gone on and on and on and on.<br/>The book relates the true tale of how the island of Malta (the stronghold of the Christian Knights who call themselves "The Religion") was besieged by the Ottoman hordes. The fictitious tale that runs parallel with this battle is about a man, Mat</span>
  <span id="freeText8051137213512087241" style="display:none">I am torn between giving four stars and five. The language and vocabulary are exquisite. It is only the incessant warring that makes one weary in the reading. The slaying and butchery would have been more striking had it not gone on and on and on and on.<br />The book relates the true tale of how the island of Malta (the stronghold of the Christian Knights who call themselves "The Religion") was besieged by the Ottoman hordes. The fictitious tale that runs parallel with this battle is about a man, Mattias Tannhauser, who has agreed to help Contessa Carla La Penautier to find her long lost bastard son. He sailed with her from Sicily to Malta at the time of war in search of the son. There is great adventure and a lot of thrills. Tim Willocks has done impeccable research and relates the story with great authority. Tim Willocks shows us the madness of religion to incite war, where each of the two sides believe they are the hand of God and the enemy is of Satan. Very interesting indeed. It is conducive to critical thought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8051137213512087241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13561790817217976438">This one is a bit too long but to tell you the truth I could not put it down. An ex-Janissary and adventurer takes up the challenge of finding a beautiful Maltese countess's illegitimate son on Malta just as the Ottomans begin their legendary siege of the Mediterranean island and commence a titanic struggle with the ruthless Knights of St. John. The battle scenes are so sickening that they must be true to life, and the intrigues involving the inquisitions, the Ottomans, the knights of St. John a</span>
  <span id="freeText13561790817217976438" style="display:none">This one is a bit too long but to tell you the truth I could not put it down. An ex-Janissary and adventurer takes up the challenge of finding a beautiful Maltese countess's illegitimate son on Malta just as the Ottomans begin their legendary siege of the Mediterranean island and commence a titanic struggle with the ruthless Knights of St. John. The battle scenes are so sickening that they must be true to life, and the intrigues involving the inquisitions, the Ottomans, the knights of St. John and sundry other parties are relentless. A really great, fat read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13561790817217976438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_605594392" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/605594392" />
    <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond"><img alt="Bryn Hammond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523356367p2/7224461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/605594392?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryn Hammond" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond">Bryn Hammond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099513568" href="/book/show/2887649-the-religion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7224461-bryn-hammond?shelf=imagined-fiction">imagined-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer605594392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8139154701657536549">From the sublime to the ridiculous. But I figure the sublime earns four stars, without taint from ridiculous content. You don't get sublime often, do you?<br/><br/>Has a big dose of swashbuckler. When the swashbuckler’s in the cockpit this isn’t ‘real-feel’ historical, because Mattias Tannhauser has been everywhere, can do everything. I was comfortable with that, I’ve swashbuckled of old, the secret is don’t try too hard to believe. <br/><br/>It’s more realistic when it comes to the war, and most of the book is war</span>
  <span id="freeText8139154701657536549" style="display:none">From the sublime to the ridiculous. But I figure the sublime earns four stars, without taint from ridiculous content. You don't get sublime often, do you?<br /><br />Has a big dose of swashbuckler. When the swashbuckler’s in the cockpit this isn’t ‘real-feel’ historical, because Mattias Tannhauser has been everywhere, can do everything. I was comfortable with that, I’ve swashbuckled of old, the secret is don’t try too hard to believe. <br /><br />It’s more realistic when it comes to the war, and most of the book is war, and that’s where he lifts to the sublime. He goes for broke on the writing. But if ever there’s a time to overwrite... other reviewers say that. How else to paint for us mad hell on earth, as is his intention, other than by his wild similes? Like this, like that – as he stretches for a phantasmagorical similar. Besides, if he didn’t, he’d only have his human pudding to talk about, and that would wear. I took the graphic depiction in my stride, never grossed out, did not suspect him of exploitation – and I can’t tell you why, when I not infrequently complain about cheap violence in histfic. Don’t know you can possibly get more violent than this one, but it’s done right – for me. As we went along I began to often think of WWI, I was transported back to my ‘WWI Lit’ class, perhaps the atmosphere or the intention was like that protest literature. On the other hand he doesn’t scrimp on the allures of war, the gallantry, the glory, the strange exhilarations: at an earlier stage I admired the book for that, for not being a 21st century anti-war tract. This novel, yes, has a stab at tackling religious war, I came to feel, as we saw sides and further sides to the subject. I liked La Valette, the head Knight of St John, even though he’s a crazed old loon: the knights of the Baptist were magnificent, the Turks were magnificent, I knew I’d fight for either cause, and Tannhauser, who tries not to be swept away, watches the glamour, of war, heroism, religion: both the glamours and the horrors absolutely presented in this book. <br /><br />Then there are the bad bits. Badder than I can politely say is the romance. Think of the worst of romances. With the indulgent fantasy of a guy. And hopelessly, hopelessly sexist. That’s a pity, because when Carla is away from Mattias she has a story of her own, in the hospital – these are the Hospitallers, sworn to ‘serve the poor’, who call the sick ‘our lords’ – a story that moved me deeply. The other woman, Amparo, seemed unusual at first – until she hooked up with Mattias, then she sank without a trace for the rest of the book. <br /><br />We have a sublime scene on the horror and the pity of war. Next we have a scene torn from a trashy romance. What a drop. I felt like the Moslems they hanged one a day, dropped from the castle walls. The strongest section, I thought, was the ‘Maltese Iliad’; the final drama was too melodrama. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8139154701657536549" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_38178021" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38178021" />
    <a title="Mandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1264730-mandy"><img alt="Mandy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38178021?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mandy" href="/user/show/1264730-mandy">Mandy</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1264730-mandy?shelf=cannot-finish">cannot-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38178021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6765167211412088867">I cannot describe how I felt about this book. As anyone who knows me can tell you, I don't usually put a book down. I will stick it out to whatever end it brings, horrifying or otherwise, as I am an avid believer in redemption, whether of the author or character(s), and I understand that characters do not always reflect the nature of the author. After all, writing (in my case, anyway) can often be a form of escape, or assuming an identity opposite of reality. In the case of The Religion, however</span>
  <span id="freeText6765167211412088867" style="display:none">I cannot describe how I felt about this book. As anyone who knows me can tell you, I don't usually put a book down. I will stick it out to whatever end it brings, horrifying or otherwise, as I am an avid believer in redemption, whether of the author or character(s), and I understand that characters do not always reflect the nature of the author. After all, writing (in my case, anyway) can often be a form of escape, or assuming an identity opposite of reality. In the case of The Religion, however, I closed it at page 47 after enduring more than I could bear of gore, boring content, and unneccessary cases of over-information, in which a character would describe obscene thoughts/feelings that were completely avoidable and could have been worded with more discretion. I simply could not finish the book. The thought of reading one more sentence was enough to make me cringe in repulsion. Perhaps this is just not a book aimed toward the female audience, and I should excuse myself, although I myself do love a good action-packed, testosterone-driven book every now and then. In conclusion, I am not a fan of The Religion, nor will I be finishing it anytime in the near or distant future. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6765167211412088867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241225684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17317156720460661685">This book was truly one of the most captivating novels I've ever read! Willocks develops his characters with great detail, and in that detail you begin to live through them as the story unravels. The battles have you feeling exhausted, the treachery has you wrathful, and the suspense is breathtaking. This is not an easy read, and it requires some patience as you get a feel for how Willocks writes and the vocabulary with which he is working. This is not a light read, rather a rich, chewy journey </span>
  <span id="freeText17317156720460661685" style="display:none">This book was truly one of the most captivating novels I've ever read! Willocks develops his characters with great detail, and in that detail you begin to live through them as the story unravels. The battles have you feeling exhausted, the treachery has you wrathful, and the suspense is breathtaking. This is not an easy read, and it requires some patience as you get a feel for how Willocks writes and the vocabulary with which he is working. This is not a light read, rather a rich, chewy journey that leaves you sate and content. This book reignited my love for reading. I hope you enjoy it as I did.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2657366052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16515326406687186522">I can't remember where exactly this book was recommended, though I suspect it might have been in a Facebook group dedicated to Grimdark fiction, a subgenre of fantasy and science fiction characterized by grittiness, morally gray characters (especially protagonists), and overall adult themes. Even though this was historical fiction, it very much felt like grimdark. <br/><br/>Overall, it was an enjoyable enough adventure, but maybe a little too over-the-top to be very good. The hero, Matthias Tannhauser, is</span>
  <span id="freeText16515326406687186522" style="display:none">I can't remember where exactly this book was recommended, though I suspect it might have been in a Facebook group dedicated to Grimdark fiction, a subgenre of fantasy and science fiction characterized by grittiness, morally gray characters (especially protagonists), and overall adult themes. Even though this was historical fiction, it very much felt like grimdark. <br /><br />Overall, it was an enjoyable enough adventure, but maybe a little too over-the-top to be very good. The hero, Matthias Tannhauser, is the sort of action movie hero who's just <i>that much</i> better than everyone, who always finds a way to win, and for whom, however many obstacles and setbacks occur, you never really doubt that he's going to win in the end. He's a great warrior, a great leader, a great military planner, a great lover, a great black-market businessman. Even when he loses, he wins, as when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[one lover dies, leaving him open to marry the other woman he loves, without ever having to resolve the love triangle, or even seriously deal with it.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. All of which makes him far less interesting than he could be--just too good at everything. <br /><br />But then, on the other side of things, Willocks does a reasonably good job of drawing more nuanced characters around Tannhauser--both the Christians and the Muslims, even though both are, at times, Tannhauser's enemies, they're shown in shades of gray rather than black and white. As much as Tannhauser is an atheist and that seems to be the author's position as well, and we certainly see some horrendous acts committed in the name of religion, we also see admirably characteristics in these characters that aren't just in spite of their religious beliefs but which flow out of them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16515326406687186522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_22444639" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22444639" />
    <a title="murph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1169440-murph"><img alt="murph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1211995530p2/1169440.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22444639?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="murph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="murph" href="/user/show/1169440-murph">murph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099493594" href="/book/show/489936.The_Religion">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22444639" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4943678118918450114">Mack Bolan goes to Malta.<br/><br/>Historical fiction has a tough challenge: weave a narrative into history that has already been written. You can't flirt with changing history, because your audience will know what must happen.<br/><br/>Tim Willocks takes a slice of history that is outside of the modern zeitgeist - the siege of Malta in 1565. This allows him to be a guide to the dramatic ebb and flow of the battle. Good historical novels educate as well as entertain.<br/><br/>Willocks in unflinching in his descriptions of th</span>
  <span id="freeText4943678118918450114" style="display:none">Mack Bolan goes to Malta.<br /><br />Historical fiction has a tough challenge: weave a narrative into history that has already been written. You can't flirt with changing history, because your audience will know what must happen.<br /><br />Tim Willocks takes a slice of history that is outside of the modern zeitgeist - the siege of Malta in 1565. This allows him to be a guide to the dramatic ebb and flow of the battle. Good historical novels educate as well as entertain.<br /><br />Willocks in unflinching in his descriptions of the brutality of the battle and in his use of the language of the period: <i>...hard on the jackhare's dust and scarcely less-swift, a bedlamite horde roared out from the purpled badlands, weapons and banners aloft, and baying like dogs in praise of their false god and his degenerate prophet.</i> Willocks is clearly enjoying himself. If you buy into it, his language can be great fun.<br /><br />Any promise this book had fades because of the central character, Tannhauser. Having made a game attempt at the primary challenge of writing historical fiction, Willocks has foundered on a challenge common to all fiction:<br /><br />He has made his main character too perfect.<br /><br />Really, picture this: How do you create a character that embodies all that is stereotypically masculine - and then try to write their story without looking ridiculous?<br /><br />Willocks creates his paragon and then dares his story to live up him. It can't. Tannhauser isn't merely heroic, he is superhuman. Women worship him, men want to be him - he is worldly, educated, a peerless warrior/lover and (in perhaps the most common mistake of any historical fiction) has an unerring ability to predict what his future holds. <br /><br />Worse, Willocks has Tannhauser playing a central role in every aspect of the Siege of Malta. He tells the lord of Malta how to defend the city. He tells the defenders where to build walls, how to defeat siege towers, when and where to raid the enemy. It's as if the Knights Hospitaliers can do nothing without first asking Tannhauser.<br /><br />There are some refreshing moments of character development (particularly with Tannhauser's nemesis, Ludovico) and great imagery throughout - but The Religion just can't get any breathing room with Mattias Tannhauser sucking the oxygen out of every page.<br /><br />Good for a distraction, but less that what it could have been.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4943678118918450114" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63742290" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63742290" />
    <a title="Jay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1618357-jay"><img alt="Jay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251571565p2/1618357.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63742290?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jay" href="/user/show/1618357-jay">Jay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781427201003" href="/book/show/1470107.The_Religion">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jay by: </span>
      Julia Towne
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1618357-jay?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63742290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2549017344743330142">I found “The Religion” strangely entertaining. I listened to the audiobook version. Initially, and for the first few CDs, I found an excessive use of proper nouns. Everything and everyone had a name, and they ran together. Thankfully, the cast was narrowed down after leaving Italy (or was it France) and setting off to Malta. Here, the characters were more fully drawn – heck, it was a siege so they had time. This is the flowery description of the attack and siege of the Catholics of Malta by the </span>
  <span id="freeText2549017344743330142" style="display:none">I found “The Religion” strangely entertaining. I listened to the audiobook version. Initially, and for the first few CDs, I found an excessive use of proper nouns. Everything and everyone had a name, and they ran together. Thankfully, the cast was narrowed down after leaving Italy (or was it France) and setting off to Malta. Here, the characters were more fully drawn – heck, it was a siege so they had time. This is the flowery description of the attack and siege of the Catholics of Malta by the armies of the Muslim Ottoman empire. There are probably more descriptions of liquids in this book than any other I’ve read, mostly bodily fluids lost during battle. Willocks specializes in gore and violence. I found myself settling into the story and interested in where it went, and it went in quite a few directions I didn’t predict. I didn’t know the outcome of this battle until I neared the end of the book, cheating a look at Wikipedia. Accurate in the highlights, certainly. Willocks draws his hero, German adventurer Tannhauser, and some other characters to superhero specifications. You could almost picture an over-muscled comic book character walking through the wasteland that was the besieged village and battling against the Turkish army that he once belonged to, with broadsword, mace, and black dagger. Of course his friend had a two-handed sword. Come to think of it, this felt like a fantasy novel with some characters plopped down into a historical fiction book. Overall, I enjoyed the results.<br /><br />The benefit of an audiobook is that it can pull you through parts of a book that might bog a reader down. This had plenty of those parts with an abundance of names in some sections, becoming almost biblical. Once down to the story, though, this was a fun, although gory, listen. The English adventurer Bohrs, though, was voiced in a forced British accent. I kept being reminded of scenes from “Ferris Bueller’s Day Off” when Ferris’ friend Cameron did his fake British accent – this sounded the same to me. I kept picturing Cameron swinging a two handed sword, and that really didn’t work well. At least it was a humorous image. Sometimes the extras in listening to an audiobook aren’t good things.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2549017344743330142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1518893414" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1518893414" />
    <a title="Rita Shire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12854323-rita-shire"><img alt="Rita Shire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370206878p2/12854323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1518893414?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rita Shire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rita Shire" href="/user/show/12854323-rita-shire">Rita Shire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1518893414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer735168974307218486">If you read this book thoroughly, it is not for the faint of heart! The setting is Malta, 1565. The Knights of Saint John the Baptist, also known as the Religion, have established themselves on the island of Malta after being defeated in the siege of Rhodes by Suleiman the Magnificient, sultan of the Ottoman Turks. The Turks have now come to Malta to annihilate The Religion. Woven into historical details of the battles, skirmishes, scouting expeditions, and atrocities is the story of the lives o</span>
  <span id="freeText735168974307218486" style="display:none">If you read this book thoroughly, it is not for the faint of heart! The setting is Malta, 1565. The Knights of Saint John the Baptist, also known as the Religion, have established themselves on the island of Malta after being defeated in the siege of Rhodes by Suleiman the Magnificient, sultan of the Ottoman Turks. The Turks have now come to Malta to annihilate The Religion. Woven into historical details of the battles, skirmishes, scouting expeditions, and atrocities is the story of the lives of Tannhauser, Bors, Carla, Amparo, and Orlandu. Willocks has a scholarly command of the weaponry, fighting techniques, and military organization of both the Turkish army and the Religion's. His knowledge of the Roman Catholic Church and the Inquisition are impeccable! His descriptions of battles and the depravation of Malta's population caused by siege warfare are as sickening as any could possibly be! In describing in such detail these monstrosities, he presents a true picture of the ugliness of war! A major theme throughout the book is the similarities in spiritual beliefs that are disguised in different languages, practices, and hierarchies,yet have such basic, fundamental, same principles. At times this book was difficult for me to read because of all the gruesome details, but I am extremely glad I did! It has turned out to be one of the best books I've ever read! The Religion is the first of the Tannhauser triology. I already have the second book, The Twelve Children of Paris, in my hand. </span>
  <a data-text-id="735168974307218486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_269895695" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269895695" />
    <a title="Allthatwander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7572275-allthatwander"><img alt="Allthatwander" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269895695?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allthatwander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allthatwander" href="/user/show/7572275-allthatwander">Allthatwander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269895695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18025050347375618169">I was given this book by my father, who had it from his father, while I was (interestingly enough) in rehab. I wasn't that thrilled at the premise, but by the time I had finished the prologue I was very invested. I loved the book. Not all of it, but enough of it to validate my giving it the five stars I think it earned. I noticed the complete lack of gloss on anything, the realism of humanity in all of its faults and failings, but also the way that it didn't try to hide that people are beautiful</span>
  <span id="freeText18025050347375618169" style="display:none">I was given this book by my father, who had it from his father, while I was (interestingly enough) in rehab. I wasn't that thrilled at the premise, but by the time I had finished the prologue I was very invested. I loved the book. Not all of it, but enough of it to validate my giving it the five stars I think it earned. I noticed the complete lack of gloss on anything, the realism of humanity in all of its faults and failings, but also the way that it didn't try to hide that people are beautiful as well as disgusting. Normally I am a fantasy kind of guy, but a little historical fiction never hurt anyone. I noticed it was compared to one of those trashy romance novels you read on the beach in another review... I have to admit, they have the right of it. Lots of sex. Still, I liked the detail payed to pretty much everything. Long story short, I enjoyed it immensely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18025050347375618169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8619628119508147654">It probably helped that I read this in Malta. The Great Siege of 1565 is one of history's great David and Goliath stories, with reminders all over the island, and this book is as good a way as any of bringing that history to life.<br/>Our hero Matthias Tannhauser is a manly man, a brawny Saxon, a former janissary, a soldier of divided loyalties. He can operate most 16th century weapons with deadly accuracy, and he speaks like Arnold Schwarzenegger (that last part is probably just in my head). He does</span>
  <span id="freeText8619628119508147654" style="display:none">It probably helped that I read this in Malta. The Great Siege of 1565 is one of history's great David and Goliath stories, with reminders all over the island, and this book is as good a way as any of bringing that history to life.<br />Our hero Matthias Tannhauser is a manly man, a brawny Saxon, a former janissary, a soldier of divided loyalties. He can operate most 16th century weapons with deadly accuracy, and he speaks like Arnold Schwarzenegger (that last part is probably just in my head). He doesn't want to go to Malta, especially not as it is being besieged by his old friends the Turks, but he is persuaded to do so by the intriguing Contessa Carla and her eccentric servant Amparo. And so he becomes unwitting witness to history.<br />If you like Bernard Cornwell's historical novels, you'll likely enjoy the testosterone-fueled adventures of Tannhauser. I'll certainly be reading the next book in the series.</span>

<div id="review_568066519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/568066519" />
    <a title="Kimberly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7251126-kimberly"><img alt="Kimberly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342428284p2/7251126.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/568066519?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kimberly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kimberly" href="/user/show/7251126-kimberly">Kimberly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7251126-kimberly?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer568066519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5590212158562972311">addendum: <br/>i've seen that people have complained about the content of this book on another site. be warned - it's gory, bloody and fairly disgusting. but hey, when you're in an army roughly the size of the population of rhode island doing battle with another army that's comparable to oh, let's say the size of at least half the population of the united states (i exagerate of course, but not by much...), it's not going to be pretty. when you see that horde coming down on you, i'm guessing it's reas</span>
  <span id="freeText5590212158562972311" style="display:none">addendum: <br />i've seen that people have complained about the content of this book on another site. be warned - it's gory, bloody and fairly disgusting. but hey, when you're in an army roughly the size of the population of rhode island doing battle with another army that's comparable to oh, let's say the size of at least half the population of the united states (i exagerate of course, but not by much...), it's not going to be pretty. when you see that horde coming down on you, i'm guessing it's reasonable for you or those around you to litterally shit yourselves. when you and and your fellow soldiers are armed with mostly swords, daggers, scimitars, pikes and poleaxes - things are going to get bloody disgusting. when you get sliced across the belly by one of those bloody blades, your insides have a tendency to uncoil out of you and though i haven't experienced that first hand, i'm guessing that after a second or two of shock, your first instinct (or that of the person tending you in the infirmary) would be to try to put that shit back inside of you where it belongs. surgery was primitive in 1565, and i don't doubt that it was often worse than what was experienced in battle. people weren't really into bathing. you used what was handy to drink water out of after the battle (usually your bloody helmet). and there's one or two sentences to do with a sexual nature that might bother the few of you out there who are still easily offended. if these things offend your sensibilities, do yourself a favor, leave this review and search for a different book. <br />now on to my review...<br /><br />actually more like 4 and almost 3/4 stars. <br />an epically good read with lots of blood and guts, sex and romance, scheming and intrigue, loyalty and betrayal, this and that (if you've read the book, you get one of the reasons i'm shorting it a quarter star - if you haven't that's your first clue).<br />the story takes place in 1565 - the siege of malta. christians fighting muslims for control of the tiny, but strategically important island. our hero is mattias tannhauser, a mercenary businessman who has experience fighting for both sides. the antagonist is fra ludivico - a man of the cloth, an inquisitor whose schemes for promotion would impress the devil himself. throw in a beautiful woman (lady carla) searching for the son who was taken from her after his birth 12 years prior on the same tiny island (along with an imposing warrior friend of tannhauser and a beguiling young woman companion to the lady) and you've got a great read. a little bit of everything for everybody. <br />if willocks could've been reigned in a bit by his editors, the book could've been a good 40 - 50 (at least) pages shorter. now don't get me wrong, i'm generally a fast reader and don't mind a 600+ page story, as long as the words and pages are necessary. however... the author tends to get bogged down here with unecessarily long discriptions and in doing so, some passages become a bit of a slog to get through and become the equivalent of taking a very strong sedative.. i probably spent close to a half a day(or more) holding the book open in front of me, but if you looked you'd have seen me sleeping. <br />that said, i did give 4 and almost 3/4 stars and highly recommend it. this book is supposed to be the first in a trilogy and i'm eagerly awaiting the next installment!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5590212158562972311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
